Commentary
This passage defines al-ja'iz and classifies its two degrees. Al-ja'iz is that which reason can conceive both as existing and as not existing. Examples include contingent substances and their accidental attributes. The definition covers all such possibilities. The two degrees: daruri (self-evident) like a body being either in motion or at rest (a dichotomous possibility), and nazari (theoretical) like God punishing a sinless obedient person or rewarding a disobedient person. The author notes that while the intellect admits these as possible after reflection, the Shari'a informs otherwise. The footnote expands on the nazari example: the mind initially finds it difficult to accept, but after reflecting on God's absolute ownership and justice (since injustice is impossible for God), it becomes clear that such actions are possible. This illustrates how nazari possibilities require deliberation.

Key Terms
الجائز
الضروري
النظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rational category 'possible' is the same as what is legally permitted or likely to happen.
Correction: Al-ja'iz is a purely rational category: whatever the mind can conceive as existent or non-existent without contradiction. It is independent of revealed law or actual occurrence.

Checkpoint
Why is punishing an obedient person considered ja'iz nazari according to the passage?
A) Because it occurs in reality
B) Because the mind initially rejects it but after reflection accepts it as possible
C) Because God is just
D) Because it is prohibited in the Shari'a

Key Takeaway
Al-ja'iz (the possible) is what the mind can accept as existing or not existing, and it is divided into what is self-evident (daruri) and what requires reflection (nazari).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God could punish you despite a lifetime of obedience, would you still worship Him the same way? What does your answer reveal about what you really trust—God's nature or your own deserving?
?
When did you last assume that God owes you something—good health, a reward, or fairness—based on your actions? Reflect on the example: is your heart at rest with God's absolute ownership, or does it demand accountability from Him?
?
The nazari possibility required you to overcome your immediate sense of justice. What is one belief about God that you hold without reflection, and are you willing to sit with the discomfort of questioning it until you reach certainty?

The Reality of Knowledge (Ma‘rifah) and Its Three Conditions
Sanusi defines knowledge (ma‘rifah) with three conditions and distinguishes it from taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three necessary components of true knowledge in Islamic theology and why evidence is essential, distinguishing it from mere imitation (taqlid).

Core Idea
True knowledge requires certainty, correspondence to reality, and evidence – not just belief.

This passage by Imam al-Sanusi lays the foundation for what counts as valid belief in kalam. Before we can discuss what we must believe about Allah, we need a clear definition of knowledge itself. The passage teaches that true knowledge is not simply having a correct belief; it must be certain, match reality, and be based on evidence – either detailed or summary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's own words set the foundation.
Notice the concise and precise style of Sanusi.
2
Identify the three components of knowledge
The passage defines knowledge (ma‘rifah) as comprising three elements: definitive judgment (jism), correspondence (muṭābaqa), and evidence (dalīl). Spot each in the text.
These are the essential conditions for valid belief.
3
Understand the two types of evidence
Sanusi explains that evidence can be detailed (tafṣīlī) – for scholars who can elaborate – or summary (ijmālī) – sufficient for laypersons. The example of Allah's existence shows summary evidence: the world as proof.
Both types satisfy the condition of having evidence.
4
See why taqlid is insufficient
A person who holds correct belief but cannot cite any evidence (saying 'I heard my father say so') does not have knowledge but taqlid (imitation). This is not enough for tawḥīd.
Taqlid lacks the essential evidence component.
5
Reflect on the consequences of doubt, assumption, and illusion
The passage states that faith based on ẓann, shakk, or wahm is invalid, and that such states in matters of creed constitute disbelief. This underscores the high standard of certainty required.
This connects to the first condition: jism (certainty).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حقيقة المعرفة هي الجزم المطابق عن دليل، فالجزم هو القطع وعدم التردد ، فمن كان إيمانه على ظن أو شك أو وهم فهو باطل، والمطابق معناه الموافق فمن كان جزمه مخالفا فهو كافر، والمقصود بالدليل الدليل التفصيلي وهو للعلماء أ والإجمالي وهو لغيرهم كجزمنا بأن الله موجود، ودليل وجوده هو هذا العالم لأنه لا بد له من فاعل، وأما الجزم المطابق إذا لم يكن معه دليل لا تفصيلي ولا إجمالي كما إذا كان إنسان يجزم بأن الله تعالى موجود، وإذا سئل عن الدليل يقول لا أدري سمعت أبي يقول ذلك فقلته ، فهذا ليس عنده معرفة وإنما عنده التقليد، وهو أخذ القول من غير معرفة دليله وهو لا يكفي في التوحيد (ط) بتصرف يسير. وحقيقة المعرفة هي الجزم المطابق للحق عن دليل، والشهادة تنبني على العلم، لا على الظن وغيره وهذا هو الموافق لمعناها اللغوي ، فالشك والظن والوهم لا تكفي في العقائد بل هي كفر.
The reality of knowledge (ma‘rifah) is definitive judgment (jism) corresponding [to truth] based on evidence (dalīl). So definitive judgment is certainty and absence of hesitation; whoever's faith is based on assumption (ẓann), doubt (shakk), or illusion (wahm) has invalid faith. Correspondence (muṭābaqa) means agreement; whoever's definitive judgment is contradictory is a disbeliever (kāfir). What is intended by evidence is the detailed evidence (dalīl tafṣīlī), which is for scholars, and the summary evidence (dalīl ijmālī), which is for others, like our definitive judgment that Allah exists; the evidence for His existence is this world because it must have a maker. As for definitive judgment that corresponds [to truth] when there is no evidence with it, neither detailed nor summary, as when a person definitively judges that Allah exists, and when asked for evidence he says 'I don't know, I heard my father say that and I said it' – this person does not have knowledge; rather he has taqlīd (imitation), which is accepting a statement without knowing its evidence, and it is not sufficient for tawḥīd (monotheism), with slight modification. And the reality of knowledge is definitive judgment corresponding to truth based on evidence, and testimony (shahāda) is built upon knowledge, not upon assumption or anything else; this is what agrees with its linguistic meaning. So doubt, assumption, and illusion are not sufficient in beliefs; rather they are disbelief.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines the true nature of knowledge (ma‘rifah) as consisting of three elements: (1) definitive judgment (jism) – certainty without hesitation; (2) correspondence (muṭābaqa) – that the judgment matches reality; (3) evidence (dalīl) – a proof that justifies the judgment. It explains that if faith is based on assumption, doubt, or illusion, it is invalid. If one's judgment contradicts the truth, it is disbelief. The evidence can be either detailed (tafṣīlī), required of scholars who can elaborate and respond to doubts, or summary (ijmālī), sufficient for the common person. Crucially, if someone has a correct belief but no evidence at all (not even summary), that is not knowledge but taqlīd (blind imitation), which is insufficient for tawḥīd. The passage then reiterates that testimony (shahāda) must be based on knowledge, not mere assumption, and that doubt, assumption, and illusion in matters of creed constitute disbelief.

Key Terms
معرفة
جزم
مطابقة
دليل
تقليد
ظن
شك
وهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Having a correct belief alone is sufficient for true knowledge.
Correction: The passage requires that the belief be certain, correspond to truth, AND be based on evidence. Without evidence, it is mere imitation (taqlid), not knowledge.

Checkpoint
What are the three components of the reality of knowledge (ma‘rifah) according to the passage?
A) Belief, action, and sincerity
B) Definitive judgment, correspondence, and evidence
C) Certainty, doubt, and assumption
D) Reason, revelation, and consensus

Key Takeaway
True knowledge in Islam requires certainty, correspondence to truth, and evidence; blind imitation (taqlid) is not valid for tawhid.

The Required Simple Proof: Common Folk vs. Theologians
Sanusi distinguishes two types of nazar: the common folk's summary proof and the theologians' detailed method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the rational consideration (nazar) required for knowledge of God is a simple, summary proof, not the detailed methodology of theologians.

Core Idea
Simple rational inference suffices; you don't need to be a theologian.

Sanusi clarifies a crucial point: what exactly is the minimal nazar that fulfills one's religious obligation? He distinguishes between a straightforward, everyday reasoning accessible to anyone and the complex arguments of kalam specialists. This passage shows that the bar is low for the common believer.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once through, then the English translation. Notice the story of al-Asma'i and the Bedouin—it illustrates the simple proof.
The Arabic is concise; read slowly to absorb the flow.
2
Identify the two types of nazar
Find where Sanusi names the two types: 'al-nazar al-ijmali' (summary) and 'al-nazar al-tafsili' (detailed). Notice which one is said to be required.
The required one is the way of the common people (tariq al-'amma).
3
Understand the Bedouin's reasoning
The Bedouin uses a simple analogy: dung indicates a camel, footprints indicate a traveler; similarly, the design of the cosmos indicates a Creator. This is a valid rational inference.
This reasoning does not require formal logic or refutation of objections.
4
Reflect on why detailed theology is not required
Sanusi explicitly states that the theologians' detailed method is not required (la yushtarat). Think about the implications: the obligation is easy to fulfill.
Theologians' work is valuable but not mandatory for every mukallaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن النظر الذي يخرج به المكلف من التقليد إلى المعرفة هو النظر على طريق العامة وهو النظر الإجمالي كما أجاب به الأعرابي الذي سأله الأصمعي بقوله : بم عرفت ريك. فقال : البعرة تدل على البعير وآثار الأقدام تدل على المسير، فسماء ذات أبراج وأرض ذات فجاج وبحور ذات أمواج ألا تدل على اللطيف الخبير. ولا يشترط النظر على طريق المتكلمين وهو النظر التفصيلي بتحرير الأدلة وترتيبها ودفع الشبه الواردة عليها.
And know that the rational consideration (nazar) by which the responsible person exits from imitation (taqlid) to knowledge (ma'rifa) is the consideration according to the way of the common people, and it is the summary consideration, as the Bedouin responded when al-Asma'i asked him: 'How did you know your Lord?' He said: 'The dung indicates the camel, and the footprints indicate the journey. So a sky with constellations, and an earth with passes, and seas with waves—do they not indicate the Subtle, the Aware?' And the consideration according to the way of the theologians (mutakallimun) is not required—it is the detailed consideration of refining proofs, arranging them, and repelling the doubts that arise against them.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage clarifies the type of rational consideration (nazar) that is legally required for the mukallaf to move from blind imitation (taqlid) to genuine knowledge (ma'rifa) of God. The required nazar is that of the common people ('amma)—a simple, summary (ijmali) proof. The author illustrates this with the famous story of al-Asma'i and a Bedouin. When asked how he knew his Lord, the Bedouin replied by analogy: camel dung indicates a camel, footprints indicate a traveler, so the grand design of the heavens, earth, and seas indicates a Creator who is subtle and aware (al-Latif al-Khabir). This intuitive inference is sufficient. In contrast, the detailed (tafsili) nazar of the theologians (mutakallimun)—which involves formulating precise arguments, ordering them logically, and defending against objections—is not required of every individual. Thus, the obligation is fulfilled by a simple rational inference, not by mastering the full science of kalam.

Key Terms
نظر
تقليد
معرفة
طريق العامة
نظر إجمالي
نظر تفصيلي
متكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that every Muslim must be a theologian (mutakallim) and master detailed arguments to know God.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the detailed method of the theologians is not required (la yushtarat). The simple, intuitive proof of the common people suffices.

Checkpoint
What is the required type of nazar according to this passage?
A) The detailed theological argument
B) The summary consideration of the common people
C) Both are required
D) No nazar is required

Key Takeaway
The minimal nazar required for knowledge of God is a simple rational inference from creation to Creator, not the detailed arguments of theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the sky, the earth, and the seas, do you genuinely see them as clear signs of Allah, or have they become ordinary sights that no longer stir your heart? If the latter, what would it take for you to recover the Bedouin's immediate recognition?
?
Are you satisfied with the simple proof from creation, or do you feel a need for more complex arguments to feel secure in your faith? What does that need say about your trust in Allah's signs?
?
If you were asked tomorrow, 'How do you know your Lord?', would your answer be as direct and certain as the Bedouin's, or would you hesitate? What is the one thing holding you back from that kind of certainty?

Knowing God's Attributes: General and Detailed
Sanusi divides knowledge of God's attributes into general and detailed under three rational categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational categories (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) that apply to God and the distinction between general and detailed knowledge of each.

Core Idea
Knowing God requires both general belief in perfection and specific knowledge of the twenty attributes.

This lesson begins by outlining the essential knowledge every Muslim must possess about God. It introduces the key division between general (ijmali) and detailed (tafsili) understanding, and organizes this knowledge under the three rational judgments: what is obligatory (wajib), impossible (mustahil), and possible (ja'iz) for God. The passage also highlights that the detailed aspect of the possible category includes the revealed beliefs (sam'iyyat), such as matters of the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: general vs. detailed for each category.
The passage uses very compressed style.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz). For each, note the general definition and the detailed requirement.
The detailed for wajib is the twenty attributes; for mustahil, their opposites; for ja'iz, sam'iyyat.
3
Reflect on the obligation
Ask yourself: why must a mukallaf know both general and detailed? How does this structure help in understanding God?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يلزم المكلف معرفته ) ، فإننا نذكره هنا على وجه الإجمال ثم نشرع في تفصيله، فهو ما يثبت في حق الله عز وجل وهو قسمان عام ومفصل ، فالعام : أن يعرف ويعتقد أن كل كمال فهو واجب لله تعالى والتفصيل أن يعرف ويعتقد بالصفات العشرين الواجبة. ويلزمه أن يعرف ما يستحيل في حق الله تعالى عموماً وتفصيلاً، فالعموم أن يعتقد أن كل نقص فهو مستحيل في حق الله تعالى، والتفصيل الاعتقاد بأضداد الصفات العشرين الواجبة (۳). ويلزمه أن يعرف ويعتقد بما يجوز في حق الله عموماً وتفصيلاً، فالعموم أن يعتقد أن فعل كل ممكن أو تركه فهو جائز في حق الله، والتفصيل بأن يعتقد العقائد السمعية كأمور الآخرة فإنها جائزة عقلا وواجبة شرع. ويلزمه شرعاً أن يعرف مثل الأقسام المتقدمة والتي هي الوجوب والاستحالة والجواز في حق الرسل ومثلهم الأنبياء، لأن كل رسول نبي ، ويجب لهم الصدق والأمانة والفطانة، ويجب للرسل إثبات التبليغ ويستحيل في حقهم الكذب والخيانة والكتمان والغفلة ،والبَله، ويجوز في حقهم الأعراض البشرية التي لا تؤدي إلى النقص في مراتبهم العلية.
And as for what is obligatory for the responsible person (mukallaf) to know, we mention it here in summary and then we will proceed to its detail. It is what is established regarding God Almighty, and it is of two types: general and detailed. The general: that he knows and believes that every perfection is obligatory for God. The detailed: that he knows and believes the twenty obligatory attributes. And it is obligatory for him to know what is impossible regarding God Almighty, in general and in detail. The general: that he believes that every imperfection is impossible regarding God Almighty. The detailed: belief in the opposites of the twenty obligatory attributes. And it is obligatory for him to know and believe what is possible regarding God Almighty in general and in detail. The general: that he believes that doing every possible thing or refraining from it is possible regarding God. The detailed: that he believes the revealed beliefs (sam'iyyat) like matters of the afterlife, for they are rationally possible and legally obligatory. And it is legally obligatory for him to know similar divisions as the preceding, which are obligation, impossibility, and possibility regarding the messengers and similarly the prophets, because every messenger is a prophet. And truthfulness, trustworthiness, and intelligence are obligatory for them, and for messengers conveyance is obligatory, and the opposite—lying, treachery, concealment, heedlessness, and dullness—is impossible regarding them, and human accidents that do not lead to deficiency in their lofty ranks are possible regarding them.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines the knowledge that is obligatory for every responsible Muslim (mukallaf). It begins by stating that the author will summarize and later detail the obligations. The knowledge concerns what is established (thabit) for God, divided into two aspects: general and detailed. The general aspect of what is obligatory (wajib) for God is to know and believe that every perfection is necessary for God. The detailed aspect is to know the twenty specific obligatory attributes (mentioned elsewhere). Similarly, what is impossible (mustahil) for God has a general requirement—to believe every imperfection is impossible—and a detailed requirement—to believe the opposites of the twenty attributes. What is possible (ja'iz) for God also has a general requirement—to believe that any possible act or omission is permissible for God—and a detailed requirement—to believe the revealed matters (sam'iyyat) like afterlife events, which are rationally possible but legally obligatory to believe. The passage then extends the same three categories (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) to the messengers and prophets. This is legally obligatory. The messengers share prophetic status. Their necessary attributes are truthfulness (sidq), trustworthiness (amana), and intelligence (fatana). For messengers specifically, conveying the message (tabligh) is obligatory. Their impossible opposites are lying, treachery, concealment, heedlessness, and dullness. Their possible attributes are human accidents (a'rad bashariyya) that do not diminish their high rank.

Key Terms
مكلف
واجب
مستحيل
جائز
صفات عشرين
عقائد سمعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowing only the general category (every perfection) suffices.
Correction: The text emphasizes that detailed knowledge of the twenty attributes is also obligatory.

Checkpoint
What is the detailed requirement regarding what is possible (ja'iz) for God?
A) To believe that every possible act is permissible
B) To believe the revealed beliefs about the afterlife
C) To believe in the twenty attributes
D) To believe that God's actions are necessary

Key Takeaway
The obligation to know God encompasses both a general recognition of His perfection and a detailed understanding of the specific attributes and their classifications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe with certainty that every perfection is obligatory for God? When you think of God's mercy, power, or wisdom, do you ever subconsciously limit or qualify His perfection in a way that contradicts this general principle? Confess any doubts and resolve to correct them.
?
This passage requires you to know not only the general category but also the detailed twenty attributes. Have you ever taken the time to learn these attributes and their opposites? If not, what is stopping you today? Commit to memorizing and understanding them.
?
The sam'iyyat — matters like the afterlife — are rationally possible and legally obligatory. Do you treat them as optional intellectual concepts or as binding truths that shape your daily choices? How often do you live as if the afterlife is unreal? Repent and realign your priorities.

Knowing the Attributes of Messengers and Prophets
Sanusi applies the three rational categories to prophets and messengers, detailing their necessary, impossible, and possible attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the same three rational judgments apply to prophets and messengers, and you will know their necessary, impossible, and possible attributes.

Core Idea
Prophets must be truthful, trustworthy, and intelligent, and they are immune to lying, treachery, and concealment.

After discussing God’s attributes, the passage extends the same framework to the messengers and prophets. It explains that every believer must know what is obligatory, impossible, and possible for them. The text enumerates the specific attributes that are necessary for them, such as truthfulness, trustworthiness, and intelligence, and the opposite attributes that are impossible, as well as permissible human qualities that do not detract from their high rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that it mirrors the structure used for God.
The passage begins with 'ويلزمه شرعاً' indicating legal obligation.
2
Identify the three categories for prophets
Find the obligatory (wajib), impossible (mustahil), and possible (ja'iz) attributes for prophets/messengers. List them.
For messengers, conveyance (tabligh) is specifically obligatory.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Why are these specific attributes necessary? How do they support the prophetic mission?
This connects to the learning objective.

Commentary
This passage defines the knowledge that is obligatory for every responsible Muslim (mukallaf). It begins by stating that the author will summarize and later detail the obligations. The knowledge concerns what is established (thabit) for God, divided into two aspects: general and detailed. The general aspect of what is obligatory (wajib) for God is to know and believe that every perfection is necessary for God. Similarly, what is impossible (mustahil) for God has a general requirement—to believe every imperfection is impossible—and a detailed requirement—to believe the opposites of the twenty attributes. What is possible (ja'iz) for God also has a general requirement—to believe that any possible act or omission is permissible for God—and a detailed requirement—to believe the revealed matters (sam'iyyat) like afterlife events, which are rationally possible but legally obligatory to believe. The passage then extends the same three categories (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) to the messengers and prophets. This is legally obligatory. The messengers share prophetic status. Their necessary attributes are truthfulness (sidq), trustworthiness (amana), and intelligence (fatana). For messengers specifically, conveying the message (tabligh) is obligatory. Their impossible opposites are lying, treachery, concealment, heedlessness, and dullness. Their possible attributes are human accidents (a'rad bashariyya) that do not diminish their high rank.

كذب
خيانة
كتمان
غفلة

أعراض بشرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think prophets could sin or be dishonest.
Correction: The text emphasizes that truthfulness, trustworthiness, and intelligence are obligatory, and their opposites are impossible.

Checkpoint
Which attribute is specifically obligatory for messengers but not explicitly for prophets?
A) Sidq (truthfulness)
B) Amana (trustworthiness)
C) Tabligh (conveyance)
D) Fatana (intelligence)

Key Takeaway
Prophets and messengers are distinguished by necessary attributes of honesty and intelligence, and they are protected from all moral and intellectual defects that would contradict their mission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to follow the prophets, but are you truthful in your speech even when it costs you? In what specific situation today have you twisted the truth, knowing that the prophets were obligated to be truthful without exception?
?
The prophets were entrusted with the greatest message and were trustworthy. Examine your own trusts—your words, your promises, your responsibilities. Where have you been negligent or dishonest this week, and what will you do to repair that breach?
?
If the prophets are protected from heedlessness about God, then your every moment of neglect in His remembrance is a departure from their path. When will you decide to replace your distractions with presence, starting right now?

General and Detailed Belief Regarding God's Acts
Sanusi distinguishes general from detailed belief regarding God's acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between general and detailed belief regarding God's acts, and why transmitted doctrines are intellectually permissible but legally obligatory.

Core Idea
Belief in God's acts has two levels: a general rule of permissibility and specific creedal obligations from revelation.

This short passage from Sanusi introduces a foundational distinction in Islamic theology: the general principle that any possible act or omission is permissible for God, versus the detailed beliefs that are required by revelation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly situating the role of reason and revelation in creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's words settle before analysis.
2
Identify the two levels of belief
Look for the terms 'العموم' (general) and 'التفصيل' (detailed). Notice how Sanusi distinguishes them.
3
Examine the ruling for detailed beliefs
The passage states that transmitted doctrines are 'جائزة عقلا' (intellectually permissible) and 'واجبة شرعا' (legally obligatory). Reflect on why both qualifications are needed.
4
Consider the theological significance
Ask yourself: Why does Sanusi start with this distinction? How does it frame the rest of the discussion on God's acts?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتفصيلاً، فالعموم أن يعتقد أن فعل كل ممكن أو تركه فهو جائز في حق الله، والتفصيل بأن يعتقد العقائد السمعية كأمور الآخرة فإنها جائزة عقلا وواجبة شرع
And in detail: the general [belief] is that one believes that every possible act or its omission is permissible (ja'iz) in relation to God, and the detailed [belief] is that one believes the transmitted doctrines (al-`aqa'id al-sam`iyya) like the matters of the afterlife, for they are permissible intellectually (ja'iz 'aqlan) and obligatory legally (wajib shar'an).
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines two levels of belief regarding God's acts: general and detailed. The general belief ('umum) is that any possible act (fi'l kull mumkin) or its omission (tark) is considered permissible (ja'iz) for God. This means nothing is inherently necessary or impossible for God regarding creation. The detailed belief (tafsil) is to hold specific transmitted doctrines (al-`aqa'id al-sam`iyya), such as matters of the afterlife. These are intellectually permissible (ja'iz 'aqlan)—meaning the intellect does not necessitate or preclude them—but they are legally obligatory (wajib shar'an) because revelation has made them mandatory to believe. The passage thus distinguishes between the generic principle of divine freedom and the specific creedal obligations derived from revelation.

Key Terms
العموم
التفصيل
جائز عقلاً
واجب شرعاً
العقائد السمعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all beliefs about God are equally intellectually necessary.
Correction: The passage clarifies that transmitted doctrines are intellectually permissible but legally required.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on matters of the afterlife ('umur al-akhira)?
A) Intellectually necessary
B) Intellectually permissible and legally obligatory
C) Intellectually impossible
D) Legally optional

Key Takeaway
The general belief is that any possible act is permissible for God, while detailed beliefs from revelation are intellectually permissible but legally obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the matters of the afterlife as if they are merely possible but not yet certain? How does the fact that they are 'legally obligatory' (wajib shar'an) change your urgency in preparing for them? What is one concrete action you will take this week to reflect that urgency?
?

How does Sanusi’s distinction between general permissibility and specific obligation challenge that assumption? Write down one belief you have been careless about and commit to studying it.
?
When you learn about a transmitted doctrine (like the punishment of the grave), do you sometimes relegate it to 'just something possible' rather than embracing it as a binding belief? What would it look like for you to treat it as legally obligatory starting today? Name one way your daily life would change.

The Four Divisions of Existents
Sanusi enumerates the four types of existents based on their relation to substrate and specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourfold classification of existents based on their need for a substrate and a specifier.

Core Idea
Existents are classified by whether they need a substrate and a specifier.

Sanusi begins by presenting a fundamental classification of all existents into four categories. This classification is based on two criteria: dependence on a substrate (mahall) and dependence on a specifier (mukhasşiş). Understanding this division is essential for grasping the unique status of Allah's essence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall structure. Then read the translation.
Notice the parallel construction with 'ghani' and 'muftaqir'.
2
Identify the two criteria
The classification uses two criteria: need for a substrate and need for a specifier. Note how each category is defined by a combination.
3
List the four categories
List each type: 1) essence of Allah, 2) attributes of Allah, 3) accident, 4) body. Write down their characteristics.
The attributes of Allah are 'ghani 'an al-mukhasşiş' but need a substrate.
4
Connect to theology
Reflect on why this classification matters: only Allah's essence is absolutely independent, showing His uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة : اعلم أن الموجودات على أربعة أقسام موجود غني عن المحل وهو ذات الله، وموجود غني عن المخصص قائم بالمحل وهو صفات الله، لأن المحل هو الذات وموجود مفتقر إلى المحل والمخصص وهو العرض، لأنه مفتقر إلى القيام بالجرم ، وموجود غني عن المحل مفتقر إلى المخصص وهو الجرم لأنه لا يحتاج إلى ذات يقوم بها ويحتاج إلى الفاعل، لأن الموجد له هو الله. واعلم أيضاً أن صفات الله لا يقال فيها مفتقرة إلى الذات بل يقال قائمة بالذات، لأن الافتقار إنما يكون إلى الغير، ولا يقال إن الصفات غير الذات، كما لا يقال إنها عين الذات.
Note: Know that existents are of four divisions: an existent independent of a substrate – that is the essence of Allah; an existent independent of a specifier, subsisting in a substrate – that is the attributes of Allah, because the substrate is the essence; an existent in need of a substrate and a specifier – that is the accident, because it needs to inhere in a body; and an existent independent of a substrate but in need of a specifier – that is the body, because it does not need an essence to subsist in but needs an agent, because its bringer into existence is Allah. Also know that the attributes of Allah are not said to be in need of the essence, but rather are said to be subsisting in the essence, because need is only towards another; and it is not said that the attributes are other than the essence, just as it is not said that they are identical to the essence.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a classification of existents into four categories based on two criteria: need for a substrate (mahall) and need for a specifier (mukhasşiş). The first is Allah's essence, which needs neither. The second is Allah's attributes, which need a substrate (the essence) but not a specifier. The third is accidents (a'rad), which need both a substrate and a specifier. The fourth is bodies (ajram), which need a specifier but not a substrate. The passage then clarifies that Allah's attributes subsist in the essence without being needy of it, and they are neither identical to the essence nor entirely other than it, following the Ash'ari middle position.

Key Terms
الموجود
غني عن المحل
مفتقر إلى المخصص
العرض
الجرم
ذات الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Essence of Allah
B) Attributes of Allah
C) Accident
D) Body

Key Takeaway
Existents are classified into four types based on their need for a substrate and a specifier, with only Allah's essence being absolutely independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that only Allah's essence is absolutely independent. Is there any created thing you rely on as if it were independent, perhaps your health, wealth, or relationships? What would it mean for you to consciously redirect that reliance to Allah alone, even for a single day?
?
The attributes of Allah subsist in His essence and do not need a specifier. How does your understanding of Allah's attributes—like His mercy or justice—affect the way you approach Him in prayer? Are you seeking the attribute or the One who possesses it?
?
Your body is a 'body' (jirm) in this classification: it does not need a substrate but depends entirely on Allah as its specifier. At this very moment, your heart beats, your lungs fill, and your mind thinks solely by His will. How can you make the awareness of this constant dependency transform your next action? Name one concrete step you will take within the next hour to acknowledge this.

The Nature of Divine Attributes
Sanusi clarifies the correct terminology for divine attributes and rejects extreme views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that divine attributes subsist in the essence without being in need of it, and are neither identical to nor other than the essence.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are not needy of the essence, they subsist in it.

After classifying existents, Sanusi addresses a crucial theological nuance: how should we speak about Allah's attributes in relation to His essence? He clarifies that attributes are not 'in need' of the essence but 'subsist' in it, and they are neither identical to the essence nor entirely other. This middle path avoids both anthropomorphism and negation of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the translation. Note the key terms.
The passage uses 'qā'imah bi al-dhāt' contrasted with 'muftaqirah'.
2
Understand the key distinction
Notice the difference between 'in need of' (iftiqar) and 'subsisting in' (qiyam bi). Why does Sanusi insist on this distinction?
3
Grasp the middle position
Sanusi rejects both identity and otherness. Reflect on why both extremes are problematic.
Attributes are distinct in meaning but not separate entities.

The Necessary Being Cannot Cease and the Impossible Cannot Exist
Sanusi proves the impossibility of the necessary being's annihilation and the impossible's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the necessary being cannot cease to exist and that the impossible cannot come into existence, and why these are fixed logical categories.

Core Idea
The necessary cannot cease; the impossible cannot exist — both are fixed by logical necessity.

This passage from Sanusi presents a concise logical argument that the two outermost categories of existence — the Necessary Being (God) and the Impossible — are permanent. The Necessary cannot cease because that would turn it into something contingent, which is contradictory. The Impossible cannot exist because any attempt would either achieve nothing or invert reality. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get a literal sense of the argument. Notice the key terms 'wajib' and 'mustahil'.
The passage is short but dense; reading it slowly helps.
2
Understand the argument about the Necessary
Sanusi argues that if the necessary being could cease to exist, it would become not-necessary (contingent). But that is impossible because a necessary being cannot be not-necessary. This is a contradiction, so cessation is impossible.
This relies on the principle that a thing cannot be both necessary and contingent.
3
Understand the argument about the Impossible
For the impossible, Sanusi considers two cases: if power and will try to annihilate it, that would be 'attaining the attained' (since it is already non-existent). If they try to bring it into existence, that would invert reality (making the impossible possible). Both are false, so the impossible cannot become existent.
The phrase 'tahsil al-hasil' means obtaining what is already obtained – a logical fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواجب لزم قلب الحقائق، فالواجب لا يمكن أن يفنى، لأنه إن فَنِي أصبح غير واجب. ويستحيل أن يكون الواجب غير واجب. وكذلك المستحيل إن تعلقتا بإعدامه لزم تحصيل الحاصل. وإن تعلقتا بإيجاده لزم قلب الحقائق وكلاهما باطل. فالمستحيل لا يقبل الوجود.
The necessary being necessitates the inversion of realities, for the necessary being cannot cease to exist, because if it ceased it would become not-necessary. And it is impossible for the necessary being to be not-necessary. Similarly, the impossible: if attached to its annihilation, it would entail attaining the attained; and if attached to its bringing into existence, it would entail inversion of realities, and both are false. So the impossible does not accept existence.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a short rational argument proving two points about the categories of necessary (wajib) and impossible (mustahil). First, the necessary being (God) cannot cease to exist, because if it did, it would become contingent (non-necessary), which is contradictory to its nature as necessary. Second, the impossible (that which cannot exist) cannot be brought into existence, because trying to annihilate it would be 'tahsil al-hasil' (obtaining what is already obtained, a logical fallacy), and trying to bring it into existence would invert the fundamental nature of reality. Both attempts are false. Therefore, the impossible is not a possible object of existence. The argument uses the principle that logical categories are fixed and any change would be a contradiction.

Key Terms
واجب
مستحيل
قلب الحقائق
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's omnipotence could change logical categories, making the impossible possible.
Correction: Logical impossibilities are not objects of power; they cannot be actualized because they entail contradictions. Sanusi shows that the impossible cannot accept existence at all.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if the necessary being (wajib) could cease to exist?
A) It would become a different necessary being.
B) It would become not-necessary (contingent).
C) Nothing would change.
D) It would become impossible.

Key Takeaway
The necessary being is eternal and cannot cease; the impossible can never exist — these are fixed by rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever live as if God's existence or attributes could change, forgetting that He is the Necessary Being who cannot cease? How does this truth confront your fears about the future or your trust in His promises?
?
If the impossible is truly impossible by rational necessity, what are some desires or expectations in your life that you need to submit to God's decree, accepting that they cannot happen?
?
The necessary being is ever-present. How does this reality challenge your moments of spiritual neglect or sin, when you act as if He is not watching or could be absent?

Kalam's Indication of the Three Categories
Sanusi classifies kalam's relation to three rational categories and gives Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how eternal divine speech indicates necessary, impossible, and contingent realities, and that this indication is eternal and de novo.

Core Idea
God's speech indicates all three rational categories, and this indication is eternal.

Sanusi begins by showing that kalam (God's eternal speech) is not limited to informing about one type of reality. It indicates necessary truths, contingent possibilities, and impossible propositions. The examples from the Quran demonstrate that the same speech covers all three. This lesson establishes that this indicating relation is eternal and not created in time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on indication
Read the first part of the source: from 'ويتعلق الكلام' to 'تنجيزي قديم'. Notice how Sanusi defines the relation as 'دلالة وإخبار'.
The Arabic term 'تنجيزي' means actualized and effective.
2
Identify the three categories and their examples
Find each category (واجب, جائز, مستحيل) and its Quranic example. Understand why each verse fits its category.
For instance, 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad' states a necessary truth about God's oneness.
3
Understand the nature of this relation
Sanusi says the relation is 'تنجيزي قديم' (de novo and eternal). Reflect on what it means for God's speech to be eternally indicating these realities.
This contrasts with created speech which would be temporal.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and try the activity to solidify your grasp.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتعلق الكلام بالواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات تعلّق دلالة وإخبار، مثال دلالة الكلام على الواجب قوله تعالى: {قُلْ هُوَ اللهُ أَحَدٌ} إلى آخر السورة، ومثال دلالته على الجائز قوله تعالى: {وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَخْتَارُ}، ومثال دلالته على المستحيل قوله تعالى: {لَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا آلِهَةٌ إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَتَا} وتعلق الكلام بالواجب والمستحيل والجائز، بمعنى دلالته عليه، تنجيزي قديم وأما الأمر والنهي فإن لم يقل برجوعهما إلى الخبر من حيث المعنى، فلهما تعلقان بأفعال المكلفين صلوحي قديم قبل وجودهم، وتنجيزي حادث بعد وجودهم.
And speech (kalam) is related to necessary things, impossible things, and contingent things by way of indication and informing. An example of speech's indication of the necessary is His statement, exalted: 'Say: He is Allah, the One' (Surah al-Ikhlas) to the end of the surah. An example of its indication of the contingent is His statement, exalted: 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (Surah al-Qasas 68). An example of its indication of the impossible is His statement, exalted: 'If there had been therein gods besides Allah, they would have been corrupted' (Surah al-Anbiya 22). And the relation of speech to the necessary, the impossible, and the contingent, in the sense of its indication of them, is de novo (tanjizi) and eternal. As for command and prohibition, if one does not say that they revert to informing in terms of meaning, then they have two relations to the actions of the obligated: a potential (suluhi) relation that is eternal before their existence, and a de novo (tanjizi) relation that is originated after their existence.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage classifies the relation of kalam (the eternal speech) to three categories: wajibat, mustahilat, and ja'izat. This relation is one of indication and informing (dilala wa ikhbar). It gives examples: the verse 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad' indicates a necessary truth about God's oneness; 'Rabbuka yakhluqu ma yasha' wa yakhtar' indicates a contingent matter (He creates as He wills); 'Law kana fihima alihatun illa Allahu lafasadata' indicates an impossibility (there cannot be multiple gods). The relation of kalam to these categories through indication is eternal and de novo (tanjizi qadim). As for command and prohibition (al-amr wa al-nahy), if they are not reducible to informing in meaning, then they have two attachments to the actions of the obligated: a potential attachment (suluhi) that is eternal before their existence, and a de novo attachment (tanjizi) that occurs after their existence (when they are addressed).

Key Terms
الواجبات
المستحيلات
الجائزات
تنجيزي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's speech only indicates contingent (possible) matters or that its indication of necessary truths is temporal.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that kalam indicates all three categories and that its indication is eternal, not created in time.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, the verse 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad' indicates which category?
A) A necessary (wajib) truth
B) A contingent (ja'iz) matter
C) An impossible (mustahil) proposition
D) All three simultaneously

Key Takeaway
The eternal speech of Allah (kalam) indicates necessary, impossible, and contingent realities, and this indicating relation is itself eternal and de novo.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad', do you truly internalize that this verse indicates a necessary truth that you cannot deny? How would your life change if you lived with the same certainty about God's oneness as you have about your own existence?
?
The verse about the impossibility of multiple gods (Surah al-Anbiya 22) is an indication of what cannot be. Are there any 'gods' in your life—like status, wealth, or desires—that you treat as having ultimate authority? What would it mean to let this verse dismantle those false claims?
?
The passage says God's speech indicates contingent realities, like His creating what He wills. Does your prayer life reflect that you truly believe your own existence and circumstances are contingent upon His will, not your own plans? What habit can you change this week to acknowledge that contingency?

The Two Attachments of Command and Prohibition
Sanusi distinguishes two attachments for command/prohibition: potential eternal and de novo temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that commands and prohibitions have two attachments to human actions: an eternal potential attachment and a temporal de novo attachment.

Core Idea
Commands and prohibitions exist eternally as potential and become actual when action is possible.

After discussing kalam's indication of the three categories, Sanusi turns to the specific case of command (amr) and prohibition (nahy). These are not simply informative; they are directives. Yet even before any human exists, God's commands and prohibitions already relate to human actions in a potential way. This lesson clarifies that two relations can be distinguished: the eternal potential (suluhi) relation and the temporal de novo (tanjizi) relation that comes into existence when the obligated person is addressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on command and prohibition
Read the second part of the source: from 'وأما الأمر والنهي' to the end. Notice the condition: 'إن لم يقل برجوعهما إلى الخبر'.
Some scholars reduce commands and prohibitions to informing, but Sanusi considers the alternative.
2
Understand the two relations: suluhi and tanjizi
Identify the potential (suluhi) relation that is eternal, and the de novo (tanjizi) relation that is originated. Understand their temporal implications.
Potential: before the obligated exists. De novo: when they are addressed.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does this distinction matter? It preserves the eternity of God's speech while accounting for the temporal nature of addressed commands.
This avoids saying that God's command begins in time.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint and do the activity to solidify the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتعلق الكلام بالواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات تعلّق دلالة وإخبار، مثال دلالة الكلام على الواجب قوله تعالى: {قُلْ هُوَ اللهُ أَحَدٌ} إلى آخر السورة، ومثال دلالته على الجائز قوله تعالى: {وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَخْتَارُ}، ومثال دلالته على المستحيل قوله تعالى: {لَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا آلِهَةٌ إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَتَا} وتعلق الكلام بالواجب والمستحيل والجائز، بمعنى دلالته عليه، تنجيزي قديم وأما الأمر والنهي فإن لم يقل برجوعهما إلى الخبر من حيث المعنى، فلهما تعلقان بأفعال المكلفين صلوحي قديم قبل وجودهم، وتنجيزي حادث بعد وجودهم.
And speech (kalam) is related to necessary things, impossible things, and contingent things by way of indication and informing. An example of speech's indication of the necessary is His statement, exalted: 'Say: He is Allah, the One' (Surah al-Ikhlas) to the end of the surah. An example of its indication of the contingent is His statement, exalted: 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (Surah al-Qasas 68). An example of its indication of the impossible is His statement, exalted: 'If there had been therein gods besides Allah, they would have been corrupted' (Surah al-Anbiya 22). And the relation of speech to the necessary, the impossible, and the contingent, in the sense of its indication of them, is de novo (tanjizi) and eternal. As for command and prohibition, if one does not say that they revert to informing in terms of meaning, then they have two relations to the actions of the obligated: a potential (suluhi) relation that is eternal before their existence, and a de novo (tanjizi) relation that is originated after their existence.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage classifies the relation of kalam (the eternal speech) to three categories: wajibat, mustahilat, and ja'izat. This relation is one of indication and informing (dilala wa ikhbar). It gives examples: the verse 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad' indicates a necessary truth about God's oneness; 'Rabbuka yakhluqu ma yasha' wa yakhtar' indicates a contingent matter (He creates as He wills); 'Law kana fihima alihatun illa Allahu lafasadata' indicates an impossibility (there cannot be multiple gods). The relation of kalam to these categories through indication is eternal and de novo (tanjizi qadim). As for command and prohibition (al-amr wa al-nahy), if they are not reducible to informing in meaning, then they have two attachments to the actions of the obligated: a potential attachment (suluhi) that is eternal before their existence, and a de novo attachment (tanjizi) that occurs after their existence (when they are addressed).

Key Terms
الأمر والنهي
صلوحي
تنجيزي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's commands and prohibitions are only temporal, beginning only when they are revealed or when humans exist.
Correction: Sanusi teaches that commands and prohibitions have an eternal potential attachment even before any human exists, while the actual de novo attachment occurs when the obligated are addressed.

Checkpoint
What is the nature of the first (suluhi) relation of command and prohibition to the actions of the obligated?
A) It is temporal and originates with the obligated
B) It is eternal and potential
C) It is de novo and created
D) It is both eternal and de novo

Key Takeaway
Commands and prohibitions have two attachments: an eternal potential attachment (suluhi) and a temporal de novo attachment (tanjizi), preserving the eternity of God's speech while accounting for temporal address.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's command has been attached to your actions from eternity, even before you existed. Does this change how you view your accountability? How would your daily choices be different if you truly felt the weight of this eternal command?
?
The de novo attachment means God's command becomes binding on you now, in this moment. Are you living as if the commands you know are merely suggestions or as if they carry the eternal authority of the Creator? What would it take for you to respond with the urgency they deserve?
?
You might treat some commands as less significant because they seem distant or 'just potential'. But the lesson shows they are rooted in eternity. Is there a command you have been neglecting because you thought it was only a temporal concern? What will you do to align with the eternal weight of that command starting today?

Weakness of Rational Proof for Divine Attributes
Al-Sawi argues that rational proof for divine hearing, sight, speech is weak and responds to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational arguments alone are insufficient to prove God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech, and how Al-Sawi defends this position.

Core Idea
Rational proof for divine hearing, sight, speech fails because perfection is relative.

In this passage, Al-Sawi addresses an important methodological point: not all divine attributes can be proven by reason alone. While the intellect can infer some attributes from contingency, hearing, sight, and speech require transmitted proof. He then considers and refutes a rational argument for these attributes, showing why analogy from contingent beings is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to follow the objection and answer. Notice the structure: claim, objection, answer.
The passage uses technical terms like 'naqlī' (transmitted) and 'aqlī' (rational).
2
Identify the objection
Find the rational argument presented: if God lacked hearing, sight, speech, He would have their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness), which are defects.
This objection assumes that absence of an attribute implies its opposite in God.
3
Understand the answer
Al-Sawi's answer: deficiency is observed in contingent beings, but we cannot analogize the eternal to the contingent. Not every perfection for a creature is a perfection for God.
The example of wife and child illustrates that what is perfection for creatures is not for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) قال الصاوي إن دليل الكلام نقلي وكذلك السمع والبصر ، لأن صنع العالم لا يتوقف على الاتصاف بها، يقصد من هذا أننا لا نعرف بالعقل من صفات الله إلا ما كان مدلولا من النظر في العالم. ثم قال إن قلت إنه يمكن أن يكون دليلها عقلياً وتقريره أن تقول لو لم يتصف بها لاتصف بضدها وهو نقص. والنقص عليه محال. أجيب بأن النقص مشاهد في الحوادث ولا يقاس القديم على الحادث، لأن كل كمال في حق الحادث لا يلزم أن يكون كمالاً في حق الله ، ألا ترى أن الزوجة والولد فإنهما كمال في حق الحادث لا القديم. فضَعُف الدليل العقلي. (ص). من هذا أن العقل لا يستطيع أن يتوصل إلى الاستدلال على السمع والبصر والكلام من مجرد ما يملكه من معلومات عن العالم من حيث حدوثه وأن الاستدلال على وجوب الاتصاف بها بالقول بأن الله إن لم يتصف بها لزم الاتصاف بأضدادها، وأضدادها نقص وهي العمى والصمم والخرس، فيلزم وجوب اتصافه تعالى بها. فإن هذا الاستدلال ليس قوياً، لأنه ليس كل ما كان كمالاً في حق الحادث يلزم أن يكون كمالا في حق القديم. وكذلك فإذا كان عدم اتصاف الحادث بالبصر يلزم منه اتصافه بالعمى، فلا يدل هذا على أن الله إذا لم يكن موصوفا بالبصر فإنه موصوف بالعمى ! لإمكان أن يكون البصر أصلاً ليس كمالاً محضاً وبالتالي فلا يجب اتصاف الله تعالى به فتأمل (س).
(1) Al-Ṣāwī said: Indeed the proof for speech is transmitted (naqlī), and likewise for hearing and sight, because the making of the world does not depend on being qualified with them. By this he intends that we do not know by the intellect from the attributes of God except what is indicated (madlūl) from reflection upon the world. Then he said: If you say that it is possible that their proof is rational, and its formulation is that you say: If He were not qualified with them, He would be qualified with their opposite, which is deficiency (naqṣ). And deficiency upon Him is impossible. The answer is: Deficiency is witnessed in contingent beings (ḥawādith), and the eternal (qadīm) cannot be analogized to the contingent, because every perfection (kamāl) with respect to the contingent does not necessarily entail that it is a perfection with respect to God. Do you not see that the wife and child are perfections with respect to the contingent, not the eternal. Thus the rational proof became weak. (Ṣ). From this: The intellect cannot arrive at inferring hearing, sight, and speech from merely what it possesses of information about the world in terms of its temporality (ḥudūth), and that inferring the necessity of being qualified with them by saying that if God is not qualified with them, then He must be qualified with their opposites, and their opposites are deficiencies—namely blindness, deafness, and muteness—then the necessity of His being qualified with them follows. Indeed this inference is not strong, because not everything that is a perfection with respect to the contingent necessarily entails that it is a perfection with respect to the Eternal. Likewise, if the lack of qualification of the contingent with sight entails its qualification with blindness, this does not indicate that if God is not qualified with sight, then He is qualified with blindness! Because it is possible that sight is originally not an absolute perfection, and consequently God's being qualified with it is not obligatory. So reflect (s).
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding the rational proof for divine attributes of hearing, sight, and speech. Al-Ṣāwī begins by stating that the proof for speech is transmitted (naqlī), not rational, and similarly for hearing and sight, because the act of creating the world does not depend on these attributes. The intellect can only know attributes that are inferred from reflection upon the contingent world. Then an objection is raised: perhaps these attributes can be proven rationally by arguing that if God were not qualified with them, He would be qualified with their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness), which are defects, and defect is impossible for God. The answer is that this argument is weak because defect is observed in contingent beings, but we cannot analogize the eternal to the contingent. What is considered perfection for a contingent being does not necessarily apply to God, e.g., having a wife and children is a perfection for a contingent being but not for God. The commentary adds that the intellect cannot independently deduce these attributes from knowledge of the world. The argument from opposites fails because it assumes that the absence of an attribute necessarily implies its opposite in God, but this is not proven because the attribute itself might not be an absolute perfection. Therefore, the rational proof is weak.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational arguments alone can prove all of God's attributes.
Correction: Some attributes like hearing, sight, speech require transmitted proof because rational inference from contingency does not necessitate them.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ṣāwī consider the rational proof for God's hearing, sight, and speech weak?
A) Because these attributes are impossible for God.
B) Because the proof relies on analogy from contingent beings, which is invalid for the eternal.
C) Because the transmitted proofs are more reliable.
D) Because these attributes are not perfections.

Key Takeaway
Rational arguments for God's hearing, sight, and speech are weak because they rely on an invalid analogy from contingent beings; therefore, transmitted proof is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you pray, do you think of God as 'hearing' in a way similar to human hearing? How should this lesson change the way you address Him in your supplications?

The First Four Impossibilities Regarding Allah
Sanusi lists the first four impossibilities for Allah with definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first four impossibilities regarding Allah and why knowing twenty specific impossibilities is necessary for proper theology.

Core Idea
Knowing what Allah cannot be is essential to knowing who He is.

Sanusi begins by explaining that while the impossibilities regarding Allah are infinite in detail, we are only required to know twenty specific ones, which are the opposites of twenty necessary attributes. He then defines the first four: non-existence, origination, passing away, and resemblance to contingent things. This lesson establishes these foundational concepts, showing how each impossibility negates a deficiency and affirms Allah's perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for familiarity, then read the literal translation. Notice the author's style: he first gives a general principle, then lists each impossibility with its definition and opposite.
The passage is dense; take your time to absorb each term.
2
Understand the general principle
The author states that impossibilities are infinite, but we must know twenty. Why? Because negating any perfection is a deficiency, and we must negate all deficiencies in general. The twenty are the opposites of the twenty necessary attributes.
This principle explains the structure of the entire section.
3
Examine the first three impossibilities
Focus on non-existence (al-'adam), origination (al-huduth), and passing away (tara al-'adam). Each is defined as the opposite of a necessary attribute. Note how each is a deficiency that cannot apply to Allah.
4
Analyze the fourth impossibility: resemblance
Resemblance to contingent things (al-mumathala) is the opposite of dissimilarity. The author elaborates: it would require Allah to occupy space, be a body or accident, or be in a direction. These are all impossible because they would contradict His transcendence.
This is the most detailed impossibility, showing the logical consequences.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you can identify the definition of resemblance. Review the passage if needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعض ما يستحيل على الله جل وعز
وقلنا بعض ما يستحيل لأن المستحيلات على الله لا نهاية لها تفصيلاً، فنفي كل كمال له تعالى نقص، يجب تنزيه الله عنه، وكل ما قدره العقل من نقص يستحيل على الله تعالى، فيجب علينا نفيه على العموم، وإنما يجب علينا معرفة عشرين مستحيلا هي أضداد العشرين الواجبة ، ويجب أيضاً أن نعرف العشرين الواجبة فكل ما ينافيها فهو مستحيل على الله تعالى.
العدم : وهو ضد الوجود وهو عبارة عن لا شيء فالوجود لله واجب والعدم مستحيل عليه تعالى.
الحدوث : وهو ضد القدم فالقدم واجب الله و الحدوث مستحيل عليه تعالى والمراد بالحدوث هو التجدد بعد العدم فكل من لم يكن ثم كان فهو الحادث.
طرو العدم : وهو ضد البقاء فالبقاء واجب الله وطرو العدم مستحيل على الله تعالى والمراد بطرو العدم الانقضاء بعد الوجود.
المماثلة للحوادث : هي ضد المخالفة الواجبة الله تعالى، والمماثلة مستحيلة على الله والمماثلة عبارة عن المشابهة للحوادث في الذات والصفات والأفعال.
فيستحيل على الله تعالى أن تأخذ ذاته قدراً من الفراغ، فيستحيل عليه تعالى أن يكون جرماً ، أو يكون عرضاً يقوم بالجرم. ويستحيل على الله تعالى أن يكون في جهة للجرم بأن يكون فوق العرش مثلاً أو تحته أو يمينه أو شماله أو أمامه أو خلفه، لأن الله تعالى لو كان في جهة للجرم لكان جرماً ولو كان جرماً لانتفت المخالفة ونفي المخالفة
Some of what is impossible for Allah, Exalted and Glorified. And we said 'some of what is impossible' because the impossibilities regarding Allah are infinite in detail, so negation of every perfection relative to Him, may He be exalted, is a deficiency, and it is necessary to declare Him free of it. And every deficiency that the mind conceives is impossible for Allah, may He be exalted, so it is obligatory upon us to negate it in general. And what is obligatory upon us is to know twenty impossibilities, which are the opposites of the twenty necessary [attributes]. And we must also know the twenty necessary [attributes], for everything that contradicts them is impossible for Allah, may He be exalted. Non-existence (al-'adam): it is the opposite of existence (al-wujud), and it is 'nothing'. So existence is necessary (wajib) for Allah, and non-existence is impossible (mustahil) upon Him, may He be exalted. Origination (al-huduth): it is the opposite of pre-eternity (al-qidam). So pre-eternity is necessary for Allah, and origination is impossible upon Him, may He be exalted. And what is meant by origination is coming into being after non-existence; so everything that was not and then was, is originated (hadith). Passing away (tara al-'adam): it is the opposite of everlastingness (al-baqa'). So everlastingness is necessary for Allah, and passing away is impossible upon Him, may He be exalted. And what is meant by passing away is perishing after existence. Resemblance to contingent things (al-mumathala li al-hawadith): it is the opposite of the necessary dissimilarity (al-mukhalafa) for Allah, may He be exalted. And resemblance is impossible upon Allah. And resemblance is the similarity to contingent things in essence, attributes, and actions. Thus it is impossible for Allah, may He be exalted, that His essence occupy any amount of space. Thus it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, to be a body (jirm), or to be an accident ('arad) subsisting in a body. And it is impossible for Allah, may He be exalted, to be in a direction relative to a body, such as being above the Throne, for example, or below it, or to its right, or to its left, or in front of it, or behind it, because if Allah, may He be exalted, were in a direction relative to a body, He would be a body, and if He were a body, dissimilarity would be negated, and the negation of dissimilarity [would follow].
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a list of impossibilities (mustahilat) regarding Allah, with definitions and explanations. It begins by stating that the author is presenting 'some' impossibilities because the detailed impossibilities are infinite. The author explains that negating any perfection from Allah is a deficiency, and Allah must be declared free from all deficiencies. However, it is obligatory to know twenty specific impossibilities, which are the opposites of the twenty necessary attributes. Knowing both sets is required. Then the author lists the first four impossibilities. 1. Non-existence (al-'adam): the opposite of existence. Since existence is necessary for Allah, non-existence is impossible. 2. Origination (al-huduth): the opposite of pre-eternity. Origination means coming into being after non-existence. Since Allah is pre-eternal, origination is impossible. 3. Passing away (tara al-'adam): the opposite of everlastingness. It means ceasing to exist after having existed. Since Allah is everlasting, passing away is impossible. 4. Resemblance to contingent things (al-mumathala li al-hawadith): the opposite of the necessary dissimilarity (mukhalafa) of Allah. Resemblance means similarity in essence, attributes, and actions. Because Allah is unlike all creation, resemblance is impossible. The author then elaborates on the fourth impossibility: if Allah resembled contingent things, He would have to occupy space, be a body or an accident, or be in a direction relative to bodies. The author refutes each: if Allah were in a direction, He would be a body; if a body, He would not be dissimilar to creation. Thus the necessity of dissimilarity is established.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think only a few specific attributes are impossible for Allah, and that the list is exhaustive.
Correction: The passage explains that impossibilities are infinite in detail; the twenty are merely the opposites of the necessary attributes that we are obliged to know.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of 'al-mumathala' (resemblance)?
A) The opposite of existence.
B) Similarity to contingent things in essence, attributes, and actions.
C) The opposite of pre-eternity.
D) The negation of all perfection.

Key Takeaway
The first four impossibilities establish that Allah cannot be non-existent, originated, perishable, or resemble any created thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that Allah cannot resemble any created thing. When you think of Allah, do you ever picture Him in your mind? What concrete step can you take today to purify your conception of Allah from any form of mental imagery?
?
Non-existence is impossible for Allah. Yet you and everything you love will one day cease to exist. If your life is not anchored in the Eternal, what are you really building? How will you shift your priorities to make Allah your ultimate focus?
?
Allah is not in any direction—not above the Throne in a physical sense. Does your heart sometimes feel that Allah is distant? How can you cultivate the certainty that He is closer to you than your jugular vein, without imagining a location?

God's Actions and Rulings Are Not for a Purpose
Refutes Mu'tazila claim that God acts for a purpose; defines actions and rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theology rejects the idea that God acts for a purpose, and how this preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Purpose implies need; Allah is free of all need and limitation.

The Mu'tazila argued that God must always do what is best for His creation (al-aslah). But does God act for a purpose? If so, that would imply need—something impossible for Allah. In this passage, the author refutes the Mu'tazila and clarifies that negating purpose does not lead to frivolity. Instead, all divine actions and rulings flow from grace or justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the main claim: purpose is impossible for God.
The passage begins with a direct contradiction of the Mu'tazila.
2
Understand the refutation of frivolity
The author argues that negating purpose does not lead to frivolity (عبث) or foolishness (سفه), because foolishness is ignorance of consequences—impossible for God.
Quranic verses are cited to support that creation is not in play.
3
Learn the definitions of actions and rulings
The note (ملاحظة) clarifies what is meant by actions (divine acts) and rulings (Sharia categories). This distinction is crucial for the argument.
Actions include giving life and death; rulings include obligatory, recommended, etc.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that God's acts and rulings are not for a purpose but are either grace (فضل) or justice (عدل). Consider how this upholds divine transcendence.
Grace and justice are attributes that do not imply need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصلح خلافا للمعتزلة، فالغرض على كل حال محال ولا يلزم من نفي الغرض العبث والسفه، لأن السفيه هو الجاهل بعواقب الأمور، وقد نفى الله السفه بقوله تعالى: أَفَحَسِبْتُمْ أَنَّمَا خَلَقْنَكُمْ عَبَثًا ﴾ [المؤمنون : ۱۱۵]، وقال تعالى: وَمَا خَلَقْنَا السَّمَاءَ وَالْأَرْضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا لَعِبِينَ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ١٦] فالغرض من أوجه المماثلة المستحيلة باعتبار الأفعال والأحكام وهو مما ينتجها مباشرة. ملاحظة : المراد بالأفعال الإيجاد والإعدام والإعزاز والإذلال والإحياء والإماتة والإغناء والإفقار، والمراد بالأحكام أحكام الشريعة وهي الواجب والمندوب والمحرم والمكروه والمباح ، فليس فعله لغرض ولا حكمه لغرض بل ذلك فضل منه أو عدل منه.
And the soundest (view) is contrary to the Mu'tazila, for purpose is impossible in any case, and no frivolity or foolishness follows from negating purpose, because the foolish one is ignorant of consequences, and Allah negated foolishness by His saying: 'Did you think that We created you in play?' [Quran 23:115], and He said: 'And We did not create the heaven and the earth and what is between them in play' [Quran 21:16]. So purpose is among the aspects of impossible similarity with respect to actions and rulings, and it is what directly produces them. Note: By actions is meant bringing into existence, causing nonexistence, honoring, humiliating, giving life, causing death, enriching, impoverishing; and by rulings is meant the rulings of the Sharia, which are obligatory, recommended, forbidden, disliked, and permissible. So His act is not for a purpose, nor is His ruling for a purpose, but that is from His grace or His justice.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-aslah (God must do what is best for creation). The author argues that purpose (gharad) is impossible for God because purpose implies need and limitation. Negating purpose does not lead to frivolity (عبث) or foolishness (سفه), because foolishness is ignorance of consequences, and God is not ignorant. He supports this with Quranic verses that deny creation in play. The author then warns that purpose is among the aspects of impossible similarity (المماثلة المستحيلة) when considering God's actions and rulings. This means attributing purpose to God would make Him resemble creatures. The note (ملاحظة) clarifies that by actions (الأفعال) is meant specific divine acts like giving life and death, and by rulings (الأحكام) is meant the legal categories of Sharia. The conclusion is that God's acts and rulings are not for any purpose but are either grace (فضل) or justice (عدل).

Key Terms
الغرض
العبث
السفه
الأصلح
الأفعال
الأحكام
فضل
عدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating purpose in God's actions leads to the conclusion that God acts arbitrarily or frivolously.
Correction: The passage distinguishes purpose from wisdom: God's actions are wise and meaningful, but not motivated by need or benefit. Frivolity is ignorance of consequences, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling regarding God's actions and rulings?
A) They are for a purpose that benefits creation.
B) They are not for a purpose but are from His grace or justice.
C) They are for a purpose but that purpose is hidden.
D) They are sometimes for a purpose and sometimes not.

Key Takeaway
God's actions and rulings are not motivated by any purpose external to Himself; they are expressions of His grace or justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When life brings hardship or unanswered prayers, do you quietly resent that Allah hasn't fulfilled the purpose you assumed He had? How does this lesson free you from that resentment and call you to trust His grace and justice instead?
?
Think of a time you obeyed a difficult command (like giving charity when you were in need). Did you secretly expect a direct reward from Allah? How does knowing that His rulings are not for a purpose (even your benefit) shift your obedience from transactional to worshipful surrender?
?
If Allah's actions are not motivated by any purpose, what does that mean for your sense of entitlement before Him? Will you now bow to His commands simply because He is the Lord, not because you perceive a benefit? What would that look like in your prayer today?
